</div></div></div></td>
</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-below hlist" style="background-color: transparent; border-color: #A2B8BF">
<ul><li><span class="nowrap"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stylised_atom_with_three_Bohr_model_orbits_and_stylised_nucleus.svg"><img alt="icon" class="noviewer" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="530" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Stylised_atom_with_three_Bohr_model_orbits_and_stylised_nucleus.svg/14px-Stylised_atom_with_three_Bohr_model_orbits_and_stylised_nucleus.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Stylised_atom_with_three_Bohr_model_orbits_and_stylised_nucleus.svg/21px-Stylised_atom_with_three_Bohr_model_orbits_and_stylised_nucleus.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Stylised_atom_with_three_Bohr_model_orbits_and_stylised_nucleus.svg/28px-Stylised_atom_with_three_Bohr_model_orbits_and_stylised_nucleus.svg.png 2x" width="14"/></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Physics" title="Portal:Physics">Physics portal</a></span></li>
<li><span class="nowrap"><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="185" data-file-width="180" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/96/Symbol_category_class.svg/16px-Symbol_category_class.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/96/Symbol_category_class.svg/23px-Symbol_category_class.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/96/Symbol_category_class.svg/31px-Symbol_category_class.svg.png 2x" title="Category" width="16"/> <a href="/wiki/Category:Classical_mechanics" title="Category:Classical mechanics">Category</a></span></li></ul></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1063604349" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Classical_mechanics" title="Template:Classical mechanics"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Classical_mechanics" title="Template talk:Classical mechanics"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Classical_mechanics&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Some of Euler's greatest successes were in solving real-world problems analytically, and in describing numerous applications of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bernoulli_numbers" title="Bernoulli numbers">Bernoulli numbers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fourier_series" title="Fourier series">Fourier series</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Euler_number" title="Euler number">Euler numbers</a>, the constants <span class="texhtml"><a href="/wiki/E_(mathematical_constant)" title="E (mathematical constant)">e</a></span> and <a href="/wiki/Pi" title="Pi"><span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span></a>, continued fractions, and integrals. He integrated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gottfried_Leibniz" title="Gottfried Leibniz">Leibniz</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Differential_calculus" title="Differential calculus">differential calculus</a> with Newton's <a href="/wiki/Method_of_Fluxions" title="Method of Fluxions">Method of Fluxions</a>, and developed tools that made it easier to apply calculus to physical problems. He made great strides in improving the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Numerical_approximation" title="Numerical approximation">numerical approximation</a> of integrals, inventing what are now known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Euler_approximations" title="Euler approximations">Euler approximations</a>. The most notable of these approximations are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Euler%27s_method" title="Euler's method">Euler's method</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-butcher_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-butcher-88">[85]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Euler%E2%80%93Maclaurin_formula" title="Euler–Maclaurin formula">Euler–Maclaurin formula</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalinger201696,_137_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalinger201696,_137-89">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerraro2008171–180Chapter_14:_Euler's_derivation_of_the_Euler–Maclaurin_summation_formula_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerraro2008171–180Chapter_14:_Euler's_derivation_of_the_Euler–Maclaurin_summation_formula-90">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mills_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-mills-91">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>Euler helped develop the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Euler%E2%80%93Bernoulli_beam_equation" title="Euler–Bernoulli beam equation">Euler–Bernoulli beam equation</a>, which became a cornerstone of engineering.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ojalvo_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-ojalvo-92">[89]</a></sup> Besides successfully applying his analytic tools to problems in <a href="/wiki/Classical_mechanics" title="Classical mechanics">classical mechanics</a>, Euler applied these techniques to celestial problems. His work in astronomy was recognized by multiple <a href="/wiki/French_Academy_of_Sciences" title="French Academy of Sciences">Paris Academy</a> Prizes over the course of his career. His accomplishments include determining with great accuracy the <a href="/wiki/Orbit" title="Orbit">orbits</a> of <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">comets</a> and other celestial bodies, understanding the nature of comets, and calculating the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_parallax" title="Solar parallax">parallax</a> of the Sun. His calculations contributed to the development of accurate <a href="/wiki/History_of_longitude" title="History of longitude">longitude tables</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yousch_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-yousch-93">[90]</a></sup>
</p><p>Euler made important contributions in <a href="/wiki/Optics" title="Optics">optics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-davidson_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-davidson-94">[91]</a></sup> He disagreed with Newton's <a href="/wiki/Corpuscular_theory_of_light" title="Corpuscular theory of light">corpuscular theory of light</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalinger1996152–153_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalinger1996152–153-95">[92]</a></sup> which was the prevailing theory of the time. His 1740s papers on optics helped ensure that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wave_theory_of_light" title="Wave theory of light">wave theory of light</a> proposed by <a href="/wiki/Christiaan_Huygens" title="Christiaan Huygens">Christiaan Huygens</a> would become the dominant mode of thought, at least until the development of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wave-particle_duality" title="Wave-particle duality">quantum theory of light</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-optics_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-optics-96">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Fluid_dynamics" title="Fluid dynamics">fluid dynamics</a>, Euler was the first to predict the phenomenon of <a href="/wiki/Cavitation" title="Cavitation">cavitation</a>, in 1754, long before its first observation in the late 19th century, and the <a href="/wiki/Euler_number_(physics)" title="Euler number (physics)">Euler number</a> used in fluid flow calculations comes from his related work on the efficiency of <a href="/wiki/Turbine" title="Turbine">turbines</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-li_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-li-97">[94]</a></sup> In 1757 he published an important set of equations for <a href="/wiki/Inviscid_flow" title="Inviscid flow">inviscid flow</a> in <a href="/wiki/Fluid_dynamics" title="Fluid dynamics">fluid dynamics</a>, that are now known as the <a href="/wiki/Euler_equations_(fluid_dynamics)" title="Euler equations (fluid dynamics)">Euler equations</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-euler2_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-euler2-98">[95]</a></sup>
</p><p>Euler is well known in <a href="/wiki/Structural_engineering" title="Structural engineering">structural engineering</a> for his formula giving <a href="/wiki/Euler%27s_critical_load" title="Euler's critical load">Euler's critical load</a>, the critical <a href="/wiki/Buckling" title="Buckling">buckling</a> load of an ideal strut, which depends only on its length and <a href="/wiki/Flexural_rigidity" title="Flexural rigidity">flexural stiffness</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGautschi200822_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGautschi200822-99">[96]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Logic">Logic</span></h3>
<p>Euler is credited with using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Closed_curve" title="Closed curve">closed curves</a> to illustrate <a href="/wiki/Syllogism" title="Syllogism">syllogistic</a> reasoning (1768). These diagrams have become known as <a href="/wiki/Euler_diagram" title="Euler diagram">Euler diagrams</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-logic_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-logic-100">[97]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Euler_Diagram.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="287" data-file-width="323" decoding="async" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Euler_Diagram.svg/170px-Euler_Diagram.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Euler_Diagram.svg/255px-Euler_Diagram.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Euler_Diagram.svg/340px-Euler_Diagram.svg.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Euler_Diagram.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An Euler diagram</div></div></div>
<p>An Euler diagram is a <a href="/wiki/Diagram" title="Diagram">diagrammatic</a> means of representing <a href="/wiki/Set_(mathematics)" title="Set (mathematics)">sets</a> and their relationships. Euler diagrams consist of simple closed curves (usually circles) in the plane that depict <a href="/wiki/Set_(mathematics)" title="Set (mathematics)">sets</a>. Each Euler curve divides the plane into two regions or "zones": the interior, which symbolically represents the <a href="/wiki/Element_(mathematics)" title="Element (mathematics)">elements</a> of the set, and the exterior, which represents all elements that are not members of the set. The sizes or shapes of the curves are not important; the significance of the diagram is in how they overlap. The spatial relationships between the regions bounded by each curve (overlap, containment or neither) corresponds to set-theoretic relationships (<a href="/wiki/Intersection_(set_theory)" title="Intersection (set theory)">intersection</a>, <a href="/wiki/Subset" title="Subset">subset</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Disjoint_sets" title="Disjoint sets">disjointness</a>). Curves whose interior zones do not intersect represent <a href="/wiki/Disjoint_sets" title="Disjoint sets">disjoint sets</a>. Two curves whose interior zones intersect represent sets that have common elements; the zone inside both curves represents the set of elements common to both sets (the <a href="/wiki/Intersection_(set_theory)" title="Intersection (set theory)">intersection</a> of the sets). A curve that is contained completely within the interior zone of another represents a <a href="/wiki/Subset" title="Subset">subset</a> of it.
</p><p>Euler diagrams (and their refinement to <a href="/wiki/Venn_diagram" title="Venn diagram">Venn diagrams</a>) were incorporated as part of instruction in <a href="/wiki/Set_theory" title="Set theory">set theory</a> as part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_math" title="New math">new math</a> movement in the 1960s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lemanski_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-lemanski-101">[98]</a></sup> Since then, they have come into wide use as a way of visualizing combinations of characteristics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rodgers_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-rodgers-102">[99]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span></h3>
<p>One of Euler's more unusual interests was the application of <a href="/wiki/Music_and_mathematics" title="Music and mathematics">mathematical ideas in music</a>. In 1739 he wrote the <i>Tentamen novae theoriae musicae</i> (<i>Attempt at a New Theory of Music</i>), hoping to eventually incorporate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Musical_theory" title="Musical theory">musical theory</a> as part of mathematics. This part of his work, however, did not receive wide attention and was once described as too mathematical for musicians and too musical for mathematicians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalinger1996144–145_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalinger1996144–145-103">[100]</a></sup> Even when dealing with music, Euler's approach is mainly mathematical,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pesic_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-pesic-104">[101]</a></sup> for instance, his introduction of <a href="/wiki/Binary_logarithm" title="Binary logarithm">binary logarithms</a> as a way of numerically describing the subdivision of <a href="/wiki/Octave" title="Octave">octaves</a> into fractional parts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tegg_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-tegg-105">[102]</a></sup> His writings on music are not particularly numerous (a few hundred pages, in his total production of about thirty thousand pages), but they reflect an early preoccupation and one that remained with him throughout his life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pesic_104-1"><a href="#cite_note-pesic-104">[101]</a></sup>
</p><p>A first point of Euler's musical theory is the definition of "genres", i.e. of possible divisions of the octave using the prime numbers 3 and 5. Euler describes 18 such genres, with the general definition 2<sup>m</sup>A, where A is the "exponent" of the genre (i.e. the sum of the exponents of 3 and 5) and 2<sup>m</sup> (where "m is an indefinite number, small or large, so long as the sounds are perceptible"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEuler1739115_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEuler1739115-106">[103]</a></sup>), expresses that the relation holds independently of the number of octaves concerned. The first genre, with A = 1, is the octave itself (or its duplicates); the second genre, 2<sup>m</sup>.3, is the octave divided by the fifth (fifth + fourth, C–G–C); the third genre is 2<sup>m</sup>.5, major third + minor sixth (C–E–C); the fourth is 2<sup>m</sup>.3<sup>2</sup>, two-fourths and a tone (C–F–B<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">♭</span></span>–C); the fifth is 2<sup>m</sup>.3.5 (C–E–G–B–C); etc. Genres 12 (2<sup>m</sup>.3<sup>3</sup>.5), 13 (2<sup>m</sup>.3<sup>2</sup>.5<sup>2</sup>) and 14 (2<sup>m</sup>.3.5<sup>3</sup>) are corrected versions of the <a href="/wiki/Genus_(music)" title="Genus (music)">diatonic, chromatic and enharmonic</a>, respectively, of the Ancients. Genre 18 (2<sup>m</sup>.3<sup>3</sup>.5<sup>2</sup>) is the "diatonico-chromatic", "used generally in all compositions",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emery_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-emery-107">[104]</a></sup> and which turns out to be identical with the system described by <a href="/wiki/Johann_Mattheson" title="Johann Mattheson">Johann Mattheson</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mattheson_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-mattheson-108">[105]</a></sup> Euler later envisaged the possibility of describing genres including the prime number 7.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-perret_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-perret-109">[106]</a></sup>
</p><p>Euler devised a specific graph, the <i>Speculum musicum</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEuler1739147_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEuler1739147-110">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-de_harmoniae_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-de_harmoniae-111">[108]</a></sup> to illustrate the diatonico-chromatic genre, and discussed paths in this graph for specific intervals, recalling his interest in the Seven Bridges of Königsberg (see <a href="#Graph_theory">above</a>). The device drew renewed interest as the <a href="/wiki/Tonnetz" title="Tonnetz">Tonnetz</a> in neo-Riemannian theory (see also <a href="/wiki/Lattice_(music)" title="Lattice (music)">Lattice (music)</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gollin_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-gollin-112">[109]</a></sup>
</p><p>Euler further used the principle of the "exponent" to propose a derivation of the <i>gradus suavitatis</i> (degree of suavity, of agreeableness) of intervals and chords from their prime factors – one must keep in mind that he considered just intonation, i.e. 1 and the prime numbers 3 and 5 only.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lindley_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-lindley-113">[110]</a></sup> Formulas have been proposed extending this system to any number of prime numbers, e.g. in the form
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle ds=\sum _{i}(k_{i}p_{i}-k_{i})+1,}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

where <i>p</i><sub><i>i</i></sub> are prime numbers and <i>k</i><sub><i>i</i></sub> their exponents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bailhache_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-bailhache-114">[111]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_philosophy_and_religious_beliefs">Personal philosophy and religious beliefs</span></h2>
<p>Euler opposed the concepts of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gottfried_Leibniz" title="Gottfried Leibniz">Leibniz's</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monadism" title="Monadism">monadism</a> and the philosophy of <a href="/wiki/Christian_Wolff_(philosopher)" title="Christian Wolff (philosopher)">Christian Wolff</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalinger1996123_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalinger1996123-115">[112]</a></sup> Euler insisted that knowledge is founded in part on the basis of precise quantitative laws, something that monadism and Wolffian science were unable to provide. Euler's religious leanings might also have had a bearing on his dislike of the doctrine; he went so far as to label Wolff's ideas as "heathen and atheistic".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wolff_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-wolff-116">[113]</a></sup>
</p><p>Euler was a religious person throughout his life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zum_werk_leonhard_21-5"><a href="#cite_note-zum_werk_leonhard-21">[18]</a></sup> Much of what is known of Euler's religious beliefs can be deduced from his <i><a href="/wiki/Letters_to_a_German_Princess" title="Letters to a German Princess">Letters to a German Princess</a></i> and an earlier work, <i>Rettung der Göttlichen Offenbahrung gegen die Einwürfe der Freygeister</i> (<i>Defense of the Divine Revelation against the Objections of the Freethinkers</i>). These works show that Euler was a devout Christian who believed the Bible to be inspired; the <i>Rettung</i> was primarily an argument for the <a href="/wiki/Biblical_inspiration" title="Biblical inspiration">divine inspiration of scripture</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-theology_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-theology-117">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ho2_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ho2-118">[115]</a></sup>
</p><p>There is a famous legend<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diderot_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-diderot-119">[116]</a></sup> inspired by Euler's arguments with secular philosophers over religion, which is set during Euler's second stint at the St. Petersburg Academy. The French philosopher <a href="/wiki/Denis_Diderot" title="Denis Diderot">Denis Diderot</a> was visiting Russia on Catherine the Great's invitation. However, the Empress was alarmed that the philosopher's arguments for <a href="/wiki/Atheism" title="Atheism">atheism</a> were influencing members of her court, and so Euler was asked to confront the Frenchman. Diderot was informed that a learned mathematician had produced a proof of the <a href="/wiki/Existence_of_God" title="Existence of God">existence of God</a>: he agreed to view the proof as it was presented in court. Euler appeared, advanced toward Diderot, and in a tone of perfect conviction announced this <a href="/wiki/Non_sequitur_(literary_device)" title="Non sequitur (literary device)">non-sequitur</a>: "Sir, <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1050945101">.mw-parser-output .sfrac{white-space:nowrap}.mw-parser-output .sfrac.tion,.mw-parser-output .sfrac .tion{display:inline-block;vertical-align:-0.5em;font-size:85%;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .sfrac .num,.mw-parser-output .sfrac .den{display:block;line-height:1em;margin:0 0.1em}.mw-parser-output .sfrac .den{border-top:1px solid}.mw-parser-output .sr-only{border:0;clip:rect(0,0,0,0);height:1px;margin:-1px;overflow:hidden;padding:0;position:absolute;width:1px}</style><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num"><i>a+b<sup>n</sup></i></span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den"><i>n</i></span></span>=<i>x</i>, hence God exists—reply!"
Diderot, to whom (says the story) all mathematics was gibberish, stood dumbstruck as peals of laughter erupted from the court. Embarrassed, he asked to leave Russia, a request that was graciously granted by the Empress. However amusing the anecdote may be, it is <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/apocryphal" title="wikt:apocryphal">apocryphal</a>, given that Diderot himself did research in mathematics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-persee_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-persee-120">[117]</a></sup>
The legend was apparently first told by Dieudonné Thiébault with embellishment by <a href="/wiki/Augustus_De_Morgan" title="Augustus De Morgan">Augustus De Morgan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diderot_119-1"><a href="#cite_note-diderot-119">[116]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Commemorations">Commemorations</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_things_named_after_Leonhard_Euler" title="List of things named after Leonhard Euler">List of things named after Leonhard Euler</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Euler-10_Swiss_Franc_banknote_(front).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1558" data-file-width="3204" decoding="async" height="107" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Euler-10_Swiss_Franc_banknote_%28front%29.jpg/220px-Euler-10_Swiss_Franc_banknote_%28front%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Euler-10_Swiss_Franc_banknote_%28front%29.jpg/330px-Euler-10_Swiss_Franc_banknote_%28front%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Euler-10_Swiss_Franc_banknote_%28front%29.jpg/440px-Euler-10_Swiss_Franc_banknote_%28front%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Euler-10_Swiss_Franc_banknote_(front).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Euler portrait on the sixth series of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swiss_Franc" title="Swiss Franc">10 Franc</a> banknote</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CHF10_7_front_horizontal.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="520" data-file-width="1074" decoding="async" height="107" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/CHF10_7_front_horizontal.jpg/220px-CHF10_7_front_horizontal.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/CHF10_7_front_horizontal.jpg/330px-CHF10_7_front_horizontal.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/CHF10_7_front_horizontal.jpg/440px-CHF10_7_front_horizontal.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CHF10_7_front_horizontal.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Euler portrait on the seventh series of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swiss_Franc" title="Swiss Franc">10 Franc</a> banknote</div></div></div>
<p>Euler was featured on both the sixth<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-swiss6_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-swiss6-121">[118]</a></sup> and seventh<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-swiss7_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-swiss7-122">[119]</a></sup> series of the Swiss 10-<a href="/wiki/Swiss_franc" title="Swiss franc">franc</a> banknote and on numerous Swiss, German, and Russian postage stamps. In 1782 he was elected a Foreign Honorary Member of the <a href="/wiki/American_Academy_of_Arts_and_Sciences" title="American Academy of Arts and Sciences">American Academy of Arts and Sciences</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aaas_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-aaas-123">[120]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroid</a> <a href="/wiki/2002_Euler" title="2002 Euler">2002 Euler</a> was named in his honour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schmadel_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-schmadel-124">[121]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Selected_bibliography">Selected bibliography</span></h2>
<p>Euler has <a href="/wiki/Contributions_of_Leonhard_Euler_to_mathematics#Works" title="Contributions of Leonhard Euler to mathematics">an extensive bibliography</a>. His books include:
</p>
<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/Mechanica" title="Mechanica">Mechanica</a></i> (1736)</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="https://www.loc.gov/item/04028085/" rel="nofollow"><i>Methodus inveniendi lineas curvas maximi minimive proprietate gaudentes, sive solutio problematis isoperimetrici latissimo sensu accepti</i> (1744)</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fraser_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-fraser-125">[122]</a></sup> (<i>A method for finding curved lines enjoying properties of maximum or minimum, or solution of isoperimetric problems in the broadest accepted sense</i>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dartm2_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-dartm2-126">[123]</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Introductio_in_analysin_infinitorum" title="Introductio in analysin infinitorum">Introductio in analysin infinitorum</a></i> (1748)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-reich_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-reich-127">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ferraro_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-ferraro-128">[125]</a></sup> (<i>Introduction to Analysis of the Infinite</i>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-revaninf_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-revaninf-129">[126]</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Institutiones_calculi_differentialis" title="Institutiones calculi differentialis">Institutiones calculi differentialis</a></i> (1755)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ferraro_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-ferraro-128">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-demidov_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-demidov-130">[127]</a></sup> (<i>Foundations of differential calculus</i>)</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Elements_of_Algebra" title="Elements of Algebra">Vollständige Anleitung zur Algebra</a></i> (1765)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ferraro_128-2"><a href="#cite_note-ferraro-128">[125]</a></sup> (<i>Elements of Algebra</i>)</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Institutiones_calculi_integralis" title="Institutiones calculi integralis">Institutiones calculi integralis</a></i> (1768–1770)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ferraro_128-3"><a href="#cite_note-ferraro-128">[125]</a></sup> (<i>Foundations of integral calculus</i>)</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Letters_to_a_German_Princess" title="Letters to a German Princess">Letters to a German Princess</a></i> (1768–1772)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunham1999xxiv–xxv_38-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunham1999xxiv–xxv-38">[35]</a></sup></li>
<li><i>Dioptrica</i>, published in three volumes beginning in 1769<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-davidson_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-davidson-94">[91]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>It took until 1830 for the bulk of Euler's posthumous works to be individually published,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kleinert_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-kleinert-131">[128]</a></sup> with an additional batch of 61 unpublished works discovered by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Paul_Heinrich_von_Fuss&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Paul Heinrich von Fuss (page does not exist)">Paul Heinrich von Fuss</a> (Euler's great-grandson and <a href="/wiki/Nicolas_Fuss" title="Nicolas Fuss">Nicolas Fuss</a>'s son) and published as a collection in 1862.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kleinert_131-1"><a href="#cite_note-kleinert-131">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fuss_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-fuss-132">[129]</a></sup> A chronological catalog of Euler's works was compiled by Swedish mathematician <a href="/wiki/Gustaf_Enestr%C3%B6m" title="Gustaf Eneström">Gustaf Eneström</a> and published from 1910 to 1913.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalinger2016ix–x_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalinger2016ix–x-133">[130]</a></sup> The catalog, known as the Eneström index, numbers Euler's works from E1 to E866.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enestrom_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-enestrom-134">[131]</a></sup> The Euler Archive was started at <a href="/wiki/Dartmouth_College" title="Dartmouth College">Dartmouth College</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-archive-start_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-archive-start-135">[132]</a></sup> before moving to the <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_Association_of_America" title="Mathematical Association of America">Mathematical Association of America</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-archive-move_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-archive-move-136">[133]</a></sup> and, most recently, to <a href="/wiki/University_of_the_Pacific_(United_States)" title="University of the Pacific (United States)">University of the Pacific</a> in 2017.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-euler_archive_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-euler_archive-137">[134]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1907, the <a href="/wiki/Swiss_Academies_of_Arts_and_Sciences" title="Swiss Academies of Arts and Sciences">Swiss Academy of Sciences</a> created the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Euler_Commission" title="Euler Commission">Euler Commission</a> and charged it with the publication of Euler’s complete works. After several delays in the 19th century,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kleinert_131-2"><a href="#cite_note-kleinert-131">[128]</a></sup> the first volume of the <i><a href="/wiki/Opera_Omnia_Leonhard_Euler" title="Opera Omnia Leonhard Euler">Opera Omnia</a></i>, was published in 1911.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pluss_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-pluss-138">[135]</a></sup> However, the discovery of new manuscripts continued to increase the magnitude of this project. Fortunately, the publication of Euler’s Opera Omnia has made steady progress, with over 70 volumes (averaging 426 pages each) published by 2006 and 80 volumes published by 2022.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-new_look_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-new_look-139">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-series_ii_done_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-series_ii_done-13">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-assad_15-3"><a href="#cite_note-assad-15">[12]</a></sup> These volumes are organized into four series. The first series compiles the works on analysis, algebra, and number theory; it consists of 29 volumes and numbers over 14,000 pages. The 31 volumes of Series II, amounting to 10,660 pages, contain the works on mechanics, astronomy, and engineering. Series III contains 12 volumes on physics. Series IV, which contains the massive amount of Euler’s correspondences, unpublished manuscripts, and notes only began compilation in 1967. The series is projected to span 16 volumes, eight volumes of which have been released as of 2022<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Leonhard_Euler&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-series_ii_done_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-series_ii_done-13">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pluss_138-1"><a href="#cite_note-pluss-138">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-assad_15-4"><a href="#cite_note-assad-15">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px"><div style="width: 235px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 230px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Acta_Eruditorum_-_II_geometria,_1744_%E2%80%93_BEIC_13411238.jpg" title="Illustration from Solutio problematis... a. 1743 propositi published in Acta Eruditorum, 1744"><img alt="Illustration from Solutio problematis... a. 1743 propositi published in Acta Eruditorum, 1744" data-file-height="1211" data-file-width="1154" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Acta_Eruditorum_-_II_geometria%2C_1744_%E2%80%93_BEIC_13411238.jpg/191px-Acta_Eruditorum_-_II_geometria%2C_1744_%E2%80%93_BEIC_13411238.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Acta_Eruditorum_-_II_geometria%2C_1744_%E2%80%93_BEIC_13411238.jpg/286px-Acta_Eruditorum_-_II_geometria%2C_1744_%E2%80%93_BEIC_13411238.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Acta_Eruditorum_-_II_geometria%2C_1744_%E2%80%93_BEIC_13411238.jpg/381px-Acta_Eruditorum_-_II_geometria%2C_1744_%E2%80%93_BEIC_13411238.jpg 2x" width="191"/></a></div></div>

</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px"><div style="width: 235px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 230px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Methodus_inveniendi_-_Leonhard_Euler_-_1744.jpg" title="The title page of Euler's Methodus inveniendi lineas curvas."><img alt="The title page of Euler's Methodus inveniendi lineas curvas." data-file-height="3763" data-file-width="2715" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Methodus_inveniendi_-_Leonhard_Euler_-_1744.jpg/144px-Methodus_inveniendi_-_Leonhard_Euler_-_1744.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Methodus_inveniendi_-_Leonhard_Euler_-_1744.jpg/216px-Methodus_inveniendi_-_Leonhard_Euler_-_1744.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Methodus_inveniendi_-_Leonhard_Euler_-_1744.jpg/288px-Methodus_inveniendi_-_Leonhard_Euler_-_1744.jpg 2x" width="144"/></a></div></div>

</p>

<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px"><div style="width: 235px">

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist reflist-lower-alpha">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-6">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The pronunciation <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/juː/: 'u' in 'cute'">juː</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/ər/: 'er' in 'letter'">ər</span></span>/</a></span></span> <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling"><span style="font-size:90%">YOO</span>-lər</i></a> is considered incorrect<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oxford_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-oxford-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-merriam_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-merriam-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-amer_heritage_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-amer_heritage-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nets,_puzzles_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-nets,_puzzles-5">[5]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-7"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-7">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">However, in the <a href="/wiki/Swiss_Standard_German" title="Swiss Standard German">Swiss variety of Standard German</a> with audible <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/r/</span>: <small></small><span class="IPA" lang="de-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Standard_German" title="Help:IPA/Standard German">[ˈɔʏlər]</a></span>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-fn2-10"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-fn2_10-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">
The quote appeared in <a href="/wiki/Guglielmo_Libri_Carucci_dalla_Sommaja" title="Guglielmo Libri Carucci dalla Sommaja">Gugliemo Libri's</a> review of a recently published collection of correspondence among eighteenth-century mathematicians: <i>"... nous rappellerions que Laplace lui même, ... ne cessait de répéter aux jeunes mathématiciens ces paroles mémorables que nous avons entendues de sa propre bouche : 'Lisez Euler, lisez Euler, c'est notre maître à tous.' "</i> [... we would recall that Laplace himself, ... never ceased to repeat to young mathematicians these memorable words that we heard from his own mouth: 'Read Euler, read Euler, he is our master in everything.]<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[137]</a></sup></span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>

style="padding:0 0.25em"></div><table class="nowraplinks navbox-subgroup" style="border-spacing:0"><tbody><tr><th class="navbox-group" scope="row" style="width:1%">Operations</th><td
